OCR Engine Comparison: Tesseract vs EasyOCR vs RapidOCR (Swahili Document)¶

This notebook compares OCR outputs generated using three different engines:

  • Tesseract
  • EasyOCR
  • RapidOCR

All outputs were produced from the same Swahili source document using Docling.

The goal is to understand how OCR engine choice affects:

  • text quality
  • structure
  • consistency
  • downstream usability

Loading OCR Outputs¶

In this section, we load the Markdown files generated by all three OCR engines.

These files contain the extracted text from the same Swahili document, processed using:

  • Tesseract
  • EasyOCR
  • RapidOCR

Loading all three versions allows us to compare how each engine handled the same input.

In [5]:
with open("../03-outputs/tesseract_outputs/Swahili-words-and-phrases-for-travelers.md", "r", encoding="utf-8") as f:
    tesseract_text = f.read()

with open("../03-outputs/easy_ocr_outputs/swahili_easyocr.md", "r", encoding="utf-8") as f:
    easyocr_text = f.read()

with open("../03-outputs/rapidocr_outputs/Swahili-words-and-phrases-for-travelers.md", "r", encoding="utf-8") as f:
    rapidocr_text = f.read()

Observation¶

The text lengths provide a quick first look at how much content each OCR engine extracted from the same document.

If the lengths are similar, it suggests that the engines captured a comparable amount of text. If they differ, that may indicate differences in extraction completeness, formatting, or OCR noise.

Length alone is not enough to determine quality, so further comparison is still needed.

Sample Output Comparison¶

Looking at a small sample helps us quickly spot differences in formatting and readability.

In [6]:
print("=== TESSERACT SAMPLE ===")
print(tesseract_text[:500])

print("\n=== EASYOCR SAMPLE ===")
print(easyocr_text[:500])

print("\n=== RAPIDOCR SAMPLE ===")
print(rapidocr_text[:500])

Observation¶

Looking at a short sample helps reveal early differences in readability, formatting, and noise across the three OCR engines.

Some outputs may appear cleaner and more structured, while others may include formatting inconsistencies or embedded non-text elements. This gives an early indication of which engine may be more suitable for downstream processing.

Line Count Comparison¶

Line count helps us understand how structured the output is.

More lines usually indicate more structured content (headings, lists, spacing).

In [7]:
def count_lines(text):
    return len(text.split("\n"))

print("Tesseract lines:", count_lines(tesseract_text))
print("EasyOCR lines:", count_lines(easyocr_text))
print("RapidOCR lines:", count_lines(rapidocr_text))

Tesseract lines: 1195
EasyOCR lines: 1195
RapidOCR lines: 1195

Observation¶

Line count helps show how each OCR engine preserved or altered the structure of the original document.

A similar number of lines may suggest similar segmentation, while noticeable differences may indicate variations in layout handling, spacing, or text grouping.

Clean Text Comparison¶

To better understand the differences between the three OCR engines, we look at a section of the document where the text is clearly extracted (without noise or embedded images).

This helps evaluate:

  • formatting
  • spacing
  • readability
In [8]:
def get_clean_sample(text, min_length=50):
    lines = [line for line in text.split("\n") if len(line.strip()) > min_length]
    return "\n".join(lines[:5])

print("=== TESSERACT CLEAN SAMPLE ===")
print(get_clean_sample(tesseract_text))

print("\n=== EASYOCR CLEAN SAMPLE ===")
print(get_clean_sample(easyocr_text))

print("\n=== RAPIDOCR CLEAN SAMPLE ===")
print(get_clean_sample(rapidocr_text))

To speak about time in Swahili, you use 'saa' (hour) and then combine the numbers above. The only difficulty is that Swahili time is counted differently than time in most other places in the world , as it's based on sunrise and sunset and not on noon and midnight. Being located close to the equator, of course sunrise and sunset in Tanzania occur around the same time every day of the year. There's a difference of 6 hours, so 0 hours isn't at midnight, but at 6 am. 5.45 pm would be saa kumi na mbili kasoro robo jioni (12 hours (6 + 6) minus a quarter in the evening).
Please realize that Tanzanians are generally less strict about time than Europeans or Américains. This isn't considered rude, especially in situations that aren't work-related but even in those, it's normal. Our guides and staff members are of course aware that timings need to be respected when dealing with our clients, but other people you meet might not be this precise. If you want to accentuate to somebody that they should be on time, you can use the word 'kamili', eg. saa moja asubuhi kamili means 7 am precisely, or use 'naomba usichelewe', meaning 'please don't be late'.

To speak about time in Swahili, you use 'saa' (hour) and then combine the numbers above. The only difficulty is that Swahili time is counted differently than time in most other places in the world , as it's based on sunrise and sunset and not on noon and midnight. Being located close to the equator, of course sunrise and sunset in Tanzania occur around the same time every day of the year. There's a difference of 6 hours, so 0 hours isn't at midnight, but at 6 am. 5.45 pm would be saa kumi na mbili kasoro robo jioni (12 hours (6 + 6) minus a quarter in the evening).
Please realize that Tanzanians are generally less strict about time than Europeans or Américains. This isn't considered rude, especially in situations that aren't work-related but even in those, it's normal. Our guides and staff members are of course aware that timings need to be respected when dealing with our clients, but other people you meet might not be this precise. If you want to accentuate to somebody that they should be on time, you can use the word 'kamili', eg. saa moja asubuhi kamili means 7 am precisely, or use 'naomba usichelewe', meaning 'please don't be late'.

To speak about time in Swahili, you use 'saa' (hour) and then combine the numbers above. The only difficulty is that Swahili time is counted differently than time in most other places in the world , as it's based on sunrise and sunset and not on noon and midnight. Being located close to the equator, of course sunrise and sunset in Tanzania occur around the same time every day of the year. There's a difference of 6 hours, so 0 hours isn't at midnight, but at 6 am. 5.45 pm would be saa kumi na mbili kasoro robo jioni (12 hours (6 + 6) minus a quarter in the evening).
Please realize that Tanzanians are generally less strict about time than Europeans or Américains. This isn't considered rude, especially in situations that aren't work-related but even in those, it's normal. Our guides and staff members are of course aware that timings need to be respected when dealing with our clients, but other people you meet might not be this precise. If you want to accentuate to somebody that they should be on time, you can use the word 'kamili', eg. saa moja asubuhi kamili means 7 am precisely, or use 'naomba usichelewe', meaning 'please don't be late'.

Findings¶

All three OCR engines produced usable outputs from the same Swahili document, but their results differed in consistency, formatting, and downstream readiness.

  • Tesseract performed well on structured extraction and preserved layout more consistently.
  • EasyOCR handled recognition effectively, but introduced slightly more formatting inconsistency.
  • RapidOCR provided a smooth and efficient pipeline, with output quality that was competitive for downstream use.

Overall, all three engines are usable, but Tesseract and RapidOCR appear more stable for structured downstream tasks in this project.

Summary Comparison Table¶

To summarize the differences observed between the three OCR engines, the table below highlights the key characteristics and trade-offs.

Feature Tesseract EasyOCR RapidOCR
Engine Type Traditional OCR Deep learning-based ONNX-based OCR pipeline
Language Used Swahili (swa) Swahili (swa) Swahili (swa)
Setup Time Fast Slower on first run Fast once models are available
Processing Speed Fast Moderate Fast
Text Accuracy Good Good, but slightly noisier Good
Structure Preservation Strong Less consistent Good
Formatting Consistency High Moderate Good
Noise Level Lower overall Slightly higher Moderate
Ease of Use Simple Requires extra setup Smooth once configured
Best Use Case Structured documents Flexible multilingual OCR Efficient OCR workflows
Notes Reliable and stable Useful but heavier Balanced and efficient
